<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1911.E2.80.9320:_Early_history">1911–20: Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1911–20: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Famous_Players_Film_Company" title="Famous Players Film Company">Famous Players Film Company</a></div>
<p>Paramount is the fifth oldest surviving film studio in the world after the French studios <a href="/wiki/Gaumont_Film_Company" title="Gaumont Film Company">Gaumont Film Company</a> (1895) and <a href="/wiki/Path%C3%A9" title="Pathé">Pathé</a> (1896), followed by the <a href="/wiki/Nordisk_Film" title="Nordisk Film">Nordisk Film</a> company (1906), and <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a> (1912).<sup id="cite_ref-Abel10_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abel10-2">[2]</a></sup> It is the last <a href="/wiki/Major_film_studio" title="Major film studio">major film studio</a> still headquartered in the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.</p>
<p>Paramount Pictures dates its existence from the 1912 founding date of the <a href="/wiki/Famous_Players_Film_Company" title="Famous Players Film Company">Famous Players Film Company</a>. Hungarian-born founder, <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Zukor" title="Adolph Zukor">Adolph Zukor</a>, who had been an early investor in <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_movie_theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickelodeon movie theatre">nickelodeons</a>, saw that movies appealed mainly to working-class immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-TIMWU2010_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIMWU2010-6">[6]</a></sup> With partners <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Frohman" title="Daniel Frohman">Daniel Frohman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Frohman" title="Charles Frohman">Charles Frohman</a> he planned to offer feature-length films that would appeal to the middle class by featuring the leading theatrical players of the time (leading to the slogan "Famous Players in Famous Plays"). By mid-1913, Famous Players had completed five films, and Zukor was on his way to success. Its first film was <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Amours_de_la_reine_%C3%89lisabeth" title="Les Amours de la reine Élisabeth">Les Amours de la reine Élisabeth</a></i>, which starred <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Bernhardt" title="Sarah Bernhardt">Sarah Bernhardt</a>.</p>
<p>That same year, another aspiring producer, <a href="/wiki/Jesse_L._Lasky" title="Jesse L. Lasky">Jesse L. Lasky</a>, opened his Lasky Feature Play Company with money borrowed from his brother-in-law, Samuel Goldfish, later known as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Goldwyn" title="Samuel Goldwyn">Samuel Goldwyn</a>. The Lasky company hired as their first employee a stage director with virtually no film experience, <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>, who would find a suitable site in Hollywood, near Los Angeles, for his first feature film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Squaw_Man_(1914_film)" title="The Squaw Man (1914 film)">The Squaw Man</a></i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_logo_1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Paramount_logo_1914.jpg/200px-Paramount_logo_1914.jpg" width="200" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Paramount_logo_1914.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="229" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_logo_1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paramount Pictures' first logo, based on a design by its founder <a href="/wiki/William_Wadsworth_Hodkinson" title="William Wadsworth Hodkinson">William Wadsworth Hodkinson</a>, used from 1917 to 1967.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Starting in 1914, both Lasky and Famous Players released their films through a start-up company, Paramount Pictures Corporation, organized early that year by a Utah theatre owner, <a href="/wiki/W._W._Hodkinson" class="mw-redirect" title="W. W. Hodkinson">W. W. Hodkinson</a>, who had bought and merged several smaller firms. Hodkinson and actor, director, producer <a href="/wiki/Hobart_Bosworth" title="Hobart Bosworth">Hobart Bosworth</a> had started production of a series of <a href="/wiki/Jack_London" title="Jack London">Jack London</a> movies. Paramount was the first successful nationwide distributor; until this time, films were sold on a statewide or regional basis which had proved costly to film producers. Also, Famous Players and Lasky were privately owned while Paramount was a corporation.</p>
<p>In 1916, Zukor maneuvered a three-way merger of his Famous Players, the Lasky Company, and Paramount. Zukor and Lasky bought Hodkinson out of Paramount, and merged the three companies into one. The new company Lasky and Zukor founded, <b><a href="/wiki/Famous_Players-Lasky" title="Famous Players-Lasky">Famous Players-Lasky</a> Corporation</b>, grew quickly, with Lasky and his partners Goldwyn and DeMille running the production side, Hiram Abrams in charge of distribution, and Zukor making great plans. With only the exhibitor-owned <a href="/wiki/First_National_Pictures" title="First National Pictures">First National</a> as a rival, Famous Players-Lasky and its "Paramount Pictures" soon dominated the business.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1921.E2.80.9330:_Rise">1921–30: Rise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1921–30: Rise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Famous_Players-Lasky" title="Famous Players-Lasky">Famous Players-Lasky</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg/220px-GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg/330px-GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg/440px-GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="619" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GrangeLasky-DeMille1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lasky's original studio (a.k.a. "The Barn") as it appeared in the mid 1920s. The Taft building, built in 1923, is visible in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because Zukor believed in stars, he signed and developed many of the leading early stars, including <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pickford" title="Mary Pickford">Mary Pickford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Clark" title="Marguerite Clark">Marguerite Clark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Frederick" title="Pauline Frederick">Pauline Frederick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Fairbanks" title="Douglas Fairbanks">Douglas Fairbanks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Swanson" title="Gloria Swanson">Gloria Swanson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_Valentino" title="Rudolph Valentino">Rudolph Valentino</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Reid" title="Wallace Reid">Wallace Reid</a>. With so many important players, Paramount was able to introduce "<a href="/wiki/Block_booking" title="Block booking">block booking</a>", which meant that an exhibitor who wanted a particular star's films had to buy a year's worth of other Paramount productions. It was this system that gave Paramount a leading position in the 1920s and 1930s, but which led the government to pursue it on <a href="/wiki/Antitrust" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitrust">antitrust</a> grounds for more than twenty years.</p>
<p>The driving force behind Paramount's rise was Zukor. Through the teens and twenties, he built the <b>Publix Theatres Corporation</b>, a chain of nearly 2,000 screens, ran two production studios (in <a href="/wiki/Astoria,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Astoria, New York">Astoria, New York</a>, now the <a href="/wiki/Kaufman_Astoria_Studios" title="Kaufman Astoria Studios">Kaufman Astoria Studios</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hollywood,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Hollywood, California">Hollywood, California</a>), and became an early investor in radio, taking a 50% interest in the new <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">Columbia Broadcasting System</a> in 1928 (selling it within a few years; this would not be the last time Paramount and CBS crossed paths).</p>
<p>In 1926, Zukor hired independent producer <a href="/wiki/B._P._Schulberg" title="B. P. Schulberg">B. P. Schulberg</a>, an unerring eye for new talent, to run the new West Coast operations. They purchased the Robert Brunton Studios, a 26-acre facility at 5451 Marathon Street for US$1 million.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1927, Famous Players-Lasky took the name <b>Paramount Famous Lasky Corporation</b>. Three years later, because of the importance of the Publix Theatres, it became <b>Paramount Publix Corporation</b>.</p>
<p>In 1928, Paramount began releasing <i><a href="/wiki/Inkwell_Imps" class="mw-redirect" title="Inkwell Imps">Inkwell Imps</a>,</i> animated cartoons produced by <a href="/wiki/Max_Fleischer" title="Max Fleischer">Max</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Fleischer" title="Dave Fleischer">Dave Fleischer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fleischer_Studios" title="Fleischer Studios">Fleischer Studios</a> in New York City. The Fleischers, veterans in the animation industry, were among the few animation producers capable of challenging the prominence of <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>. The Paramount newsreel series <a href="/wiki/Paramount_News" title="Paramount News">Paramount News</a> ran from 1927 to 1957. Paramount was also one of the first Hollywood studios to release what were known at that time as "<a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">talkies</a>", and in 1929, released their first musical, <i><a href="/wiki/Innocents_of_Paris" title="Innocents of Paris">Innocents of Paris</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Richard_A._Whiting" title="Richard A. Whiting">Richard A. Whiting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leo_Robin" title="Leo Robin">Leo Robin</a> composed the score for the film; <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Chevalier" title="Maurice Chevalier">Maurice Chevalier</a> starred and sung the most famous song from the film, "<a href="/wiki/Louise_(Maurice_Chevalier_song)" title="Louise (Maurice Chevalier song)">Louise</a>".</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Publix.2C_Balaban_and_Katz.2C_Loew.27s_competition_and_wonder_theaters">Publix, Balaban and Katz, Loew's competition and wonder theaters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Publix, Balaban and Katz, Loew's competition and wonder theaters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By acquiring the successful <a href="/wiki/Balaban_%26_Katz" class="mw-redirect" title="Balaban &amp; Katz">Balaban &amp; Katz</a> chain in 1926, Zukor gained the services of <a href="/wiki/Barney_Balaban" title="Barney Balaban">Barney Balaban</a> (who would eventually become Paramount's president in 1936), his brother <a href="/wiki/A._J._Balaban" title="A. J. Balaban">A. J. Balaban</a> (who would eventually supervise all stage production nationwide and produce talkie shorts), and their partner Sam Katz (who would run the Paramount-Publix theatre chain in New York City from the thirty-five-story Paramount Theatre Building on <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">Times Square</a>).</p>
<p>Balaban and Katz had developed the Wonder Theater concept, first publicized around 1918 and sponsored by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jadeja_Motion_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jadeja Motion Pictures (page does not exist)">Jadeja Motion Pictures</a> in Chicago. The Chicago Theater was created as a very ornate theater and advertised as a "wonder theater." When Publix acquired Balaban, they embarked on a project to expand the wonder theaters, and starting building in New York in 1927. While Balaban and Public were dominant in Chicago, <a href="/wiki/Loew%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Loew's">Loew's</a> was the big player in New York, and did not want the Publix theaters to overshadow theirs. The two companies brokered a non-competition deal for New York and Chicago, and Loew's took over the New York area projects, developing five <a href="/wiki/Loew%27s_Wonder_Theaters" class="mw-redirect" title="Loew's Wonder Theaters">wonder theaters</a>. Publix continued Balaban's wonder theater development in its home area.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1931.E2.80.9340:_Receivership">1931–40: Receivership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1931–40: Receivership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Eventually, Zukor shed most of his early partners; the Frohman brothers, Hodkinson and Goldwyn were out by 1917 while Lasky hung on until 1932, when, blamed for the near-collapse of Paramount in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Depression</a> years, he too was tossed out. Zukor's over-expansion and use of overvalued Paramount stock for purchases led the company into receivership in 1933. A bank-mandated reorganization team, led by <a href="/wiki/John_D._Hertz" title="John D. Hertz">John Hertz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_Kahn" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto Kahn">Otto Kahn</a> kept the company intact, and, miraculously, Zukor was kept on. In 1935, Paramount-Publix went bankrupt. In 1936, Barney Balaban became president, and Zukor was bumped up to chairman of the board. In this role, Zukor reorganized the company as <b>Paramount Pictures, Inc.</b> and was able to successfully bring the studio out of bankruptcy.</p>
<p>As always, Paramount films continued to emphasize stars; in the 1920s there were Swanson, Valentino, and <a href="/wiki/Clara_Bow" title="Clara Bow">Clara Bow</a>. By the 1930s, talkies brought in a range of powerful new draws: <a href="/wiki/Miriam_Hopkins" title="Miriam Hopkins">Miriam Hopkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marlene_Dietrich" title="Marlene Dietrich">Marlene Dietrich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">Mae West</a>, <a href="/wiki/W.C._Fields" class="mw-redirect" title="W.C. Fields">W.C. Fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeanette_MacDonald" title="Jeanette MacDonald">Jeanette MacDonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudette_Colbert" title="Claudette Colbert">Claudette Colbert</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Marx_Brothers" title="Marx Brothers">Marx Brothers</a> (whose first two films were shot at Paramount's <a href="/wiki/Astoria,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Astoria, New York">Astoria, New York</a>, studio), <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Lamour" title="Dorothy Lamour">Dorothy Lamour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carole_Lombard" title="Carole Lombard">Carole Lombard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a>, band leader <a href="/wiki/Shep_Fields" title="Shep Fields">Shep Fields</a>, famous Argentine tango singer <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Gardel" title="Carlos Gardel">Carlos Gardel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Cooper" title="Gary Cooper">Gary Cooper</a> among them.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In this period Paramount can truly be described as a movie factory, turning out sixty to seventy pictures a year. Such were the benefits of having a huge theater chain to fill, and of block booking to persuade other chains to go along. In 1933, <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">Mae West</a> would also add greatly to Paramount's success with her suggestive movies <i><a href="/wiki/She_Done_Him_Wrong" title="She Done Him Wrong">She Done Him Wrong</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/I%27m_No_Angel" title="I'm No Angel">I'm No Angel</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> However, the sex appeal West gave in these movies would also lead to the enforcement of the <a href="/wiki/Production_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="Production Code">Production Code</a>, as the newly formed organization the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Legion_of_Decency" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Legion of Decency">Catholic Legion of Decency</a> threatened a boycott if it was not enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paramount cartoons produced by Fleischer Studios continued to be successful, with characters such as <a href="/wiki/Betty_Boop" title="Betty Boop">Betty Boop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Popeye_the_Sailor" class="mw-redirect" title="Popeye the Sailor">Popeye the Sailor</a> becoming widely successful. One Fleischer series, <i><a href="/wiki/Screen_Songs" title="Screen Songs">Screen Songs</a></i>, featured live-action music stars under contract to Paramount hosting sing-alongs of popular songs. The animation studio would rebound with <a href="/wiki/Popeye" title="Popeye">Popeye</a>, and in 1935, polls showed that Popeye was even more popular than <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> After an unsuccessful expansion into feature films, as well as the fact that Max and Dave Fleischer were no longer speaking to one another, Fleischer Studios was acquired by Paramount, which renamed the operation <a href="/wiki/Famous_Studios" title="Famous Studios">Famous Studios</a>. That incarnation of the animation studio continued cartoon production until 1967, but has been historically dismissed as having largely failed to maintain the artistic acclaim the Fleischer brothers achieved under their management.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ParamountLogo1930s.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/94/ParamountLogo1930s.JPG" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="166" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ParamountLogo1930s.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The original Paramount logo seen on its 1930s films and <i><a href="/wiki/Popeye" title="Popeye">Popeye</a></i> shorts.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1941.E2.80.9350:_United_States_v._Paramount_Pictures.2C_Inc.">1941–50: United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1941–50: United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1940, Paramount agreed to a government-instituted consent decree: block booking and "pre-selling" (the practice of collecting up-front money for films not yet in production) would end. Immediately, Paramount cut back on production, from seventy-one pictures to a more modest nineteen annually in the war years.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Still, with more new stars like <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Ladd" title="Alan Ladd">Alan Ladd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veronica_Lake" title="Veronica Lake">Veronica Lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paulette_Goddard" title="Paulette Goddard">Paulette Goddard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Betty_Hutton" title="Betty Hutton">Betty Hutton</a>, and with war-time attendance at astronomical numbers, Paramount and the other integrated studio-theatre combines made more money than ever. At this, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> decided to reopen their case against the five integrated studios. Paramount also had a monopoly over <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> movie theaters through subsidiary company United Detroit Theaters as well.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This led to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> decision <a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Paramount_Pictures,_Inc." title="United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.">United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.</a> (1948) holding that movie studios could not also own movie theater chains. This decision broke up Adolph Zukor's creation and effectively brought an end to the classic Hollywood <a href="/wiki/Studio_system" title="Studio system">studio system</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1951.E2.80.9366:_Split_and_after">1951–66: Split and after</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1951–66: Split and after">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the separation of production and exhibition forced by the U.S. Supreme Court, Paramount Pictures Inc. was split in two.<sup id="cite_ref-Nelmes_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelmes-17">[17]</a></sup> Paramount Pictures Corporation was formed to be the production distribution company, with the 1,500-screen theater chain handed to the new <a href="/wiki/United_Paramount_Theaters" class="mw-redirect" title="United Paramount Theaters">United Paramount Theaters</a> on December 31, 1949. <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Goldenson" title="Leonard Goldenson">Leonard Goldenson</a>, who had headed the chain since 1938, remained as the new company's president. The Balaban and Katz theatre division was spun off with UPT; its trademark eventually became the property of the Balaban and Katz Historical Foundation. The Foundation has recently acquired ownership of the Famous Players Trademark. Cash-rich and controlling prime downtown real estate, Goldenson began looking for investments. Barred from film-making by prior anti-trust rulings, he acquired the struggling <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> television network in February 1953, leading it first to financial health, and eventually, in the mid-1970s, to first place in the national Nielsen ratings, before selling out to <a href="/wiki/Capital_Cities_Communications" title="Capital Cities Communications">Capital Cities</a> in 1985 (Capital Cities would eventually sell out, in turn, to <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a> in 1996). United Paramount Theaters was renamed ABC Theaters in 1965 and was sold to businessman Henry Plitt in 1974. The movie theater chain was renamed Plitt Theaters. In 1985, <a href="/wiki/Cineplex_Odeon_Corporation" title="Cineplex Odeon Corporation">Cineplex Odeon Corporation</a> merged with Plitt. In later years, Paramount's TV division would develop a strong relationship with ABC, providing many hit series to the network.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_DuMont_Network">The DuMont Network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The DuMont Network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Paramount Pictures had been an early backer of television, launching experimental stations in 1939 in Los Angeles and Chicago. The Los Angeles station eventually became <a href="/wiki/KTLA" title="KTLA">KTLA</a>, the first commercial station on the West Coast. The Chicago station got a commercial license as WBKB in 1943, but was sold to UPT along with Balaban &amp; Katz in 1948 and was eventually resold to CBS as <a href="/wiki/WBBM-TV" title="WBBM-TV">WBBM-TV</a>.</p>
<p>In 1938, Paramount bought a stake in television manufacturer <a href="/wiki/DuMont_Laboratories" title="DuMont Laboratories">DuMont Laboratories</a>. Through this stake, it became a minority owner of the <a href="/wiki/DuMont_Television_Network" title="DuMont Television Network">DuMont Television Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hess1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hess1-18">[18]</a></sup> Also Paramount launched its own network, <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television_Network" title="Paramount Television Network">Paramount Television Network</a>, in 1948 through its television unit, Television Productions, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-Schatz_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schatz-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paramount management planned to acquire additional <a href="/wiki/Owned-and-operated_station" title="Owned-and-operated station">owned-and-operated stations</a> ("O&amp;Os"); the company applied to the FCC for additional stations in San Francisco, Detroit, and Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-Browne_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-20">[20]</a></sup> The FCC, however, denied Paramount's applications. A few years earlier, the federal regulator had placed a five-station cap on all television networks: no network was allowed to own more than five <a href="/wiki/VHF" class="mw-redirect" title="VHF">VHF</a> television stations. Paramount was hampered by its minority stake in the DuMont Television Network. Although both DuMont and Paramount executives stated that the companies were separate, the FCC ruled that Paramount's partial ownership of DuMont meant that DuMont and Paramount were in theory branches of the same company. Since DuMont owned three television stations and Paramount owned two, the federal agency ruled neither network could acquire additional television stations. The FCC requested that Paramount relinquish its stake in DuMont, but Paramount refused.<sup id="cite_ref-Browne_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-20">[20]</a></sup> According to television historian William Boddy, "Paramount's checkered anti-trust history" helped convince the FCC that Paramount controlled DuMont.<sup id="cite_ref-Boddy_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boddy-21">[21]</a></sup> Both DuMont and Paramount Television Network suffered as a result, with neither company able to acquire five O&amp;Os. Meanwhile, CBS, ABC, and NBC had each acquired the maximum of five stations by the mid-1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-BrooksandMarsh_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BrooksandMarsh-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>When ABC accepted a merger offer from UPT in 1953, DuMont quickly realized that ABC now had more resources than it could possibly hope to match. It quickly reached an agreement in principle to merge with ABC.<sup id="cite_ref-Bergmann5_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergmann5-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1951, Paramount bought a stake in <a href="/wiki/Telemeter_(pay_television)" title="Telemeter (pay television)">International Telemeter</a>, an experimental pay TV service which operated with a coin inserted into a box. The service began operating in Palm Springs, California on November 27, 1953, but due to pressure from the FCC, the service ended on May 15, 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the loss of the theater chain, Paramount Pictures went into a decline, cutting studio-backed production, releasing its contract players, and making production deals with independents. By the mid-1950s, all the great names were gone; only <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>, associated with Paramount since 1913, kept making pictures in the grand old style. Despite Paramount's losses, DeMille would, however, give the studio some relief and create his most successful film at Paramount, a 1956 <a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1956_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1956 film)">remake</a> of his 1923 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1923_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1923 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> DeMille died in 1959. Like some other studios, Paramount saw little value in its film library, and sold 764 of its pre-1948 films to <a href="/wiki/MCA_Inc." title="MCA Inc.">MCA Inc.</a> (known today as <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios Inc.</a>) in February 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1966.E2.80.9370:_Early_Gulf.2BWestern_era">1966–70: Early Gulf+Western era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1966–70: Early Gulf+Western era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_Pictures_(Gulf%2BWestern)_logo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Paramount_Pictures_%28Gulf%2BWestern%29_logo.jpg/250px-Paramount_Pictures_%28Gulf%2BWestern%29_logo.jpg" width="250" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Paramount_Pictures_%28Gulf%2BWestern%29_logo.jpg/375px-Paramount_Pictures_%28Gulf%2BWestern%29_logo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b5/Paramount_Pictures_%28Gulf%2BWestern%29_logo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="245" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_Pictures_(Gulf%2BWestern)_logo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paramount's logo from 1953–1975. The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Gulf+Western</a> byline was introduced following the company's purchase of Paramount. The variant shown here was used in the first three <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones" title="Indiana Jones">Indiana Jones</a> films, the first of which was released in 1981.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the early 1960s, Paramount's future was doubtful. The high-risk movie business was wobbly; the theater chain was long gone; investments in DuMont and in early pay-television came to nothing; and the Golden Age of Hollywood had just ended, even the flagship Paramount building in Times Square was sold to raise cash, as was KTLA (sold to <a href="/wiki/Gene_Autry" title="Gene Autry">Gene Autry</a> in 1964 for a then-phenomenal $12.5&#160;million). Their only remaining successful property at that point was <a href="/wiki/Dot_Records" title="Dot Records">Dot Records</a>, which Paramount had acquired in 1957, and even its profits started declining by the middle of the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Founding father Adolph Zukor (born in 1873) was still chairman emeritus; he referred to chairman Barney Balaban (born 1888) as "the boy." Such aged leadership was incapable of keeping up with the changing times, and in 1966, a sinking Paramount was sold to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bluhdorn" title="Charles Bluhdorn">Charles Bluhdorn</a>'s industrial conglomerate, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Gulf + Western Industries Corporation</a>. Bluhdorn immediately put his stamp on the studio, installing a virtually unknown producer named <a href="/wiki/Robert_Evans_(producer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Evans (producer)">Robert Evans</a> as head of production. Despite some rough times, Evans held the job for eight years, restoring Paramount's reputation for commercial success with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Odd_Couple_(film)" title="The Odd Couple (film)">The Odd Couple</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rosemary%27s_Baby_(film)" title="Rosemary's Baby (film)">Rosemary's Baby</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Story_(1970_film)" title="Love Story (1970 film)">Love Story</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather" title="The Godfather">The Godfather</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Chinatown_(1974_film)" title="Chinatown (1974 film)">Chinatown</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/3_Days_of_the_Condor" class="mw-redirect" title="3 Days of the Condor">3 Days of the Condor</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gulf + Western Industries also bought the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Desilu_Productions" title="Desilu Productions">Desilu</a> television studio (once the lot of <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Pictures</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Lucille_Ball" title="Lucille Ball">Lucille Ball</a> in 1967. Using some of Desilu's established shows such as <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series" title="Star Trek: The Original Series">Star Trek</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mission:_Impossible" title="Mission: Impossible">Mission: Impossible</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mannix" title="Mannix">Mannix</a></i> as a foot in the door at the networks, the newly reincorporated <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television" title="Paramount Television">Paramount Television</a> eventually became known as a specialist in half-hour situation comedies.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1971.E2.80.9380:_CIC_formation_and_high-concept_era">1971–80: CIC formation and high-concept era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1971–80: CIC formation and high-concept era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1970, Paramount teamed with <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Cinema_International_Corporation" title="Cinema International Corporation">Cinema International Corporation</a>, a new company that would distribute films by the two studios outside the United States. <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a> would become a partner in the mid-1970s. Both Paramount and CIC entered the video market with Paramount Home Video (now <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Home_Media_Distribution" title="Paramount Home Media Distribution">Paramount Home Entertainment</a>) and <a href="/wiki/CIC_Video" title="CIC Video">CIC Video</a>, respectively.</p>
<p>Robert Evans abandoned his position as head of production in 1974; his successor, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Sylbert" title="Richard Sylbert">Richard Sylbert</a>, proved to be too literary and too tasteful for Gulf + Western's Bluhdorn. By 1976, a new, television-trained team was in place headed by <a href="/wiki/Barry_Diller" title="Barry Diller">Barry Diller</a> and his "Killer-Dillers", as they were called by admirers or "Dillettes" as they were called by detractors. These associates, made up of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Eisner" title="Michael Eisner">Michael Eisner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Katzenberg" title="Jeffrey Katzenberg">Jeffrey Katzenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dawn_Steel" title="Dawn Steel">Dawn Steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Don_Simpson" title="Don Simpson">Don Simpson</a> would each go on and head up major movie studios of their own later in their careers.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_(1968).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3b/Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_%281968%29.svg/250px-Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_%281968%29.svg.png" width="250" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3b/Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_%281968%29.svg/375px-Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_%281968%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3b/Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_%281968%29.svg/500px-Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_%281968%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="163" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_Pictures_print_logo_(1968).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paramount's print logo with the Viacom byline. This logo has been used since 1968, with minor variations, all of which reflected corporate changes. The new byline was introduced in 2010.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Paramount specialty was now simpler. "<a href="/wiki/High_concept" class="mw-redirect" title="High concept">High concept</a>" pictures such as <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Fever" title="Saturday Night Fever">Saturday Night Fever</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Grease_(film)" title="Grease (film)">Grease</a></i> hit big, hit hard and hit fast all over the world,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and Diller's television background led him to propose one of his longest-standing ideas to the board: <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television_Service" title="Paramount Television Service">Paramount Television Service</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fourth_television_network" title="Fourth television network">fourth commercial network</a>. Paramount Pictures purchased the <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Television_Network" title="Hughes Television Network">Hughes Television Network</a> (HTN) including its satellite time in planning for PTVS in 1976. Paramount sold HTN to Madison Square Garden in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-ap_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap-31">[31]</a></sup> But Diller believed strongly in the concept, and so took his fourth-network idea with him when he moved to <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> in 1984, where Fox's then freshly installed proprietor, <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a> was a more interested listener.</p>
<p>However, the television division would be playing catch-up for over a decade after Diller's departure in 1984 before launching its own television network – <a href="/wiki/UPN" title="UPN">UPN</a> – in 1995. Lasting eleven years before being merged with <a href="/wiki/The_WB" title="The WB">The WB</a> network to become <a href="/wiki/The_CW" title="The CW">The CW</a> in 2006, UPN would feature many of the shows it originally produced for other networks, and would take numerous gambles on series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Voyager" title="Star Trek: Voyager">Star Trek: Voyager</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Enterprise" title="Star Trek: Enterprise">Star Trek: Enterprise</a></i> that would have otherwise either gone direct-to-cable or become <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_syndication" title="Broadcast syndication">first-run syndication</a> to independent stations across the country (as <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Deep_Space_Nine" title="Star Trek: Deep Space Nine">Star Trek: Deep Space Nine</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Next_Generation" title="Star Trek: The Next Generation">Star Trek: The Next Generation</a></i> were).</p>
<p>Paramount Pictures was not connected to either <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Records" title="Paramount Records">Paramount Records</a> (1910s-1935) or <a href="/wiki/ABC_Records" title="ABC Records">ABC-Paramount Records</a> (1955–66) until it purchased the rights to use the name (but not the latter's catalog) in the late 1960s. The <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Records_(1969)" title="Paramount Records (1969)">Paramount</a> name was used for soundtrack albums and some pop re-issues from the <a href="/wiki/Dot_Records" title="Dot Records">Dot Records</a> catalog which Paramount had acquired in 1957. By 1970, Dot had become an all-country label<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and in 1974, Paramount sold all of its record holdings to <a href="/wiki/ABC_Records" title="ABC Records">ABC Records</a>, which in turn was sold to <a href="/wiki/MCA_Inc." title="MCA Inc.">MCA</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">Universal Music Group</a>) in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980.E2.80.9394:_Continual_success">1980–94: Continual success</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1980–94: Continual success">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Paramount's successful run of pictures extended into the 1980s and 1990s, generating hits like <i><a href="/wiki/Airplane!" title="Airplane!">Airplane!</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/American_Gigolo" title="American Gigolo">American Gigolo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ordinary_People" title="Ordinary People">Ordinary People</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/An_Officer_and_a_Gentleman" title="An Officer and a Gentleman">An Officer and a Gentleman</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Flashdance" title="Flashdance">Flashdance</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Terms_of_Endearment" title="Terms of Endearment">Terms of Endearment</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Footloose_(1984_film)" title="Footloose (1984 film)">Footloose</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pretty_in_Pink" title="Pretty in Pink">Pretty in Pink</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Top_Gun" title="Top Gun">Top Gun</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crocodile_Dundee" title="Crocodile Dundee">Crocodile Dundee</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fatal_Attraction" title="Fatal Attraction">Fatal Attraction</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_(1990_film)" title="Ghost (1990 film)">Ghost</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_the_13th_(1980_film)" title="Friday the 13th (1980 film)">Friday the 13th</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Slasher_film" title="Slasher film">slasher</a> series, as well as teaming up with <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm" title="Lucasfilm">Lucasfilm</a> to create the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones_(franchise)" title="Indiana Jones (franchise)"><i>Indiana Jones</i> franchise</a>. Other examples are the <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a></i> film series and a string of films starring comedian <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Murphy" title="Eddie Murphy">Eddie Murphy</a> like <i><a href="/wiki/Trading_Places" title="Trading Places">Trading Places</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Coming_to_America" title="Coming to America">Coming to America</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills_Cop" title="Beverly Hills Cop">Beverly Hills Cop</a></i> and its sequels. While the emphasis was decidedly on the commercial, there were occasional less commercial but more artistic and intellectual efforts like <i><a href="/wiki/I%27m_Dancing_as_Fast_as_I_Can" title="I'm Dancing as Fast as I Can">I'm Dancing as Fast as I Can</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_City_(1980_film)" title="Atlantic City (1980 film)">Atlantic City</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Reds_(film)" title="Reds (film)">Reds</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Witness_(1985_film)" title="Witness (1985 film)">Witness</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Children_of_a_Lesser_God" title="Children of a Lesser God">Children of a Lesser God</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Accused_(1988_film)" title="The Accused (1988 film)">The Accused</a></i>. During this period, responsibility for running the studio passed from Eisner and Katzenberg to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Mancuso,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Mancuso, Sr.">Frank Mancuso, Sr.</a> (1984) and <a href="/wiki/Ned_Tanen" title="Ned Tanen">Ned Tanen</a> (1984) to <a href="/wiki/Stanley_R._Jaffe" title="Stanley R. Jaffe">Stanley R. Jaffe</a> (1991) and <a href="/wiki/Sherry_Lansing" title="Sherry Lansing">Sherry Lansing</a> (1992). More so than most, Paramount's slate of films included many remakes and television spinoffs; while sometimes commercially successful, there have been few compelling films of the kind that once made Paramount the industry leader.</p>
<p>On August 25, 1983, Paramount Studios caught fire. Two or three sound stages and four outdoor sets were destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Charles Bluhdorn died unexpectedly, his successor Martin Davis dumped all of G+W's industrial, mining, and sugar-growing subsidiaries and refocused the company, renaming it <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Paramount Communications</a> in 1989. With the influx of cash from the sale of G+W's industrial properties in the mid-1980s, Paramount bought a string of television stations and <a href="/wiki/KECO_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="KECO Entertainment">KECO Entertainment</a>'s theme park operations, renaming them <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Parks" title="Paramount Parks">Paramount Parks</a>. These parks included <a href="/wiki/California%27s_Great_America" title="California's Great America">Paramount's Great America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada%27s_Wonderland" title="Canada's Wonderland">Paramount Canada's Wonderland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carowinds" title="Carowinds">Paramount's Carowinds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kings_Dominion" title="Kings Dominion">Paramount's Kings Dominion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kings_Island" title="Kings Island">Paramount's Kings Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1993, <a href="/wiki/Sumner_Redstone" title="Sumner Redstone">Sumner Redstone</a>'s entertainment conglomerate <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(1971%E2%80%932005)" class="mw-redirect" title="Viacom (1971–2005)">Viacom</a> made a bid for a merger with Paramount Communications; this quickly escalated into a bidding war with <a href="/wiki/Barry_Diller" title="Barry Diller">Barry Diller</a>'s <a href="/wiki/QVC" title="QVC">QVC</a>. But Viacom prevailed, ultimately paying $10&#160;billion for the Paramount holdings. Viacom and Paramount had planned to merge as early as 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paramount is the last major film studio located in Hollywood proper. When Paramount moved to its present home in 1927, it was in the heart of the film community. Since then, former next-door neighbor <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO</a> closed up shop in 1957 (Paramount ultimately absorbed their former lot); <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> (whose old Sunset Boulevard studio was sold to Paramount in 1949 as a home for <a href="/wiki/KTLA" title="KTLA">KTLA</a>) moved to <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_Los_Angeles_County,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Burbank, Los Angeles County, California">Burbank</a> in 1930; <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia</a> joined Warners in Burbank in 1973 then moved again to <a href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California" title="Culver City, California">Culver City</a> in 1989; and the Pickford-Fairbanks-Goldwyn-United Artists lot, after a lively history, has been turned into a <a href="/wiki/Post-production" title="Post-production">post-production</a> and music-scoring facility for Warners, known simply as "The Lot". For a time the semi-industrial neighborhood around Paramount was in decline, but has now come back. The recently refurbished studio has come to symbolize Hollywood for many visitors, and its studio tour is a popular attraction.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1994.E2.80.932005:_Dolgen.2FLansing_and_.22old.22_Viacom_era">1994–2005: Dolgen/Lansing and "old" Viacom era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1994–2005: Dolgen/Lansing and &quot;old&quot; Viacom era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(original)" title="Viacom (original)">Viacom (original)</a></div>
<p>During this time period, Paramount Pictures went under the guidance of Jonathan Dolgen, chairman and <a href="/wiki/Sherry_Lansing" title="Sherry Lansing">Sherry Lansing</a>, president.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> During their administration over Paramount, the studio had an extremely successful period of films with two of Paramount's ten highest-grossing films being produced during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The most successful of these films, <i><a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">Titanic</a></i>, a joint partnership with <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lightstorm_Entertainment" title="Lightstorm Entertainment">Lightstorm Entertainment</a> became the highest-grossing film up to that time, grossing over $1.8&#160;billion worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Also during this time, three Paramount Pictures films won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> for Best Picture; <i>Titanic, Braveheart</i>, and <i>Forrest Gump</i>.</p>
<p>Paramount's most important property, however, was <i>Star Trek</i>. Studio executives had begun to call it "the franchise" in the 1980s due to its reliable revenue, and other studios envied its "untouchable and unduplicatable" success. By 1998 <i>Star Trek</i> TV shows, movies, books, videotapes, and licensing provided so much of the studio's profit that "it is not possible to spend any reasonable amount of time at Paramount and not be aware of [its] presence"; filming for <i>Star Trek: Voyager</i> and <i>Star Trek: Deep Space Nine</i> required up to nine of the largest of the studio's 36 <a href="/wiki/Sound_stage" title="Sound stage">sound stages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-meehan2005_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meehan2005-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-poe1998_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poe1998-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:49–50,54</sup></p>
<p>In 1995, Viacom and <a href="/wiki/Chris-Craft_Industries" title="Chris-Craft Industries">Chris-Craft Industries</a>' <a href="/wiki/BHC_Communications#United_Television" title="BHC Communications">United Television</a> launched <a href="/wiki/UPN" title="UPN">United Paramount Network</a> (UPN) with <i>Star Trek: Voyager</i> as its flagship series, fulfilling Barry Diller's plan for a Paramount network from 25 years earlier. In 1999, Viacom bought out United Television's interests, and handed responsibility for the start-up network to the newly acquired <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> unit, which Viacom bought in 1999 – an ironic confluence of events as Paramount had once invested in CBS, and Viacom had once been the syndication arm of CBS as well.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> During this period the studio acquired some 30 TV stations to support the UPN network as well acquiring and merging in the assets of Republic Pictures, Spelling Television and Viacom Television, almost doubling the size of the studio's TV library. The TV division produced the dominant prime time show for the decade in <i>Frasier</i> as well as such long running hits as NCIS and <i>Becker</i> and the dominant prime time magazine show <i>Entertainment Tonight.</i></p>
<p>During this period, Paramount and its related subsidiaries and affiliates, operating under the name "Viacom Entertainment Group" also included the fourth largest group of theme parks in the United States and Canada which in addition to traditional rides and attractions launched numerous successful location-based entertainment units including a long running "Star Trek" attraction at the Las Vegas Hilton. Famous Music – the company's celebrated music publishing arm almost doubled in size and developed artists including Pink, Bush, Green Day as well as catalog favorites including Duke Ellington and Henry Mancini. The Paramount/Viacom licensing group under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Tom_McGrath_(media_executive)" title="Tom McGrath (media executive)">Tom McGrath</a> created the "Cheers" franchise bars and restaurants and a chain of restaurants borrowing from the studio's Academy Award-winning film "Forrest Gump" – <i>The Bubba Gump Shrimp Company</i>. Through the combined efforts of Famous Music and the studio over ten "Broadway" musicals were created including Irving Berlin's <i>White Christmas</i>, <i>Footloose, Saturday Night Fever</i>, Andrew Lloyd Weber's <i>Sunset Boulevard</i> among others. The Company's international arm, United International Pictures (UIP), was the dominant distributor internationally for ten straight years representing Paramount, Universal and MGM. Simon and Schuster became part of the Viacom Entertainment Group emerging as the US' dominant trade book publisher.</p>
<p>In 2002, Paramount, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_Motion_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures">Buena Vista Distribution</a>, <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Pictures Entertainment">Sony Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Cinema_Initiatives" title="Digital Cinema Initiatives">Digital Cinema Initiatives</a>. Operating under a waiver form the anti-trust law, the studios combined under the leadership of Paramount Chief Operating Officer <a href="/wiki/Tom_McGrath_(media_executive)" title="Tom McGrath (media executive)">Tom McGrath</a> to develop technical standards for the eventual introduction of digital film projection – replacing the now 100-year-old film technology.<sup id="cite_ref-dcimovies.com_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcimovies.com-46">[46]</a></sup> DCI was created "to establish and document voluntary specifications for an open architecture for digital cinema that ensures a uniform and high level of technical performance, reliability and quality control."<sup id="cite_ref-dcimovies.com_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcimovies.com-46">[46]</a></sup> McGrath also headed up Paramount's initiative for the creation and launch of the Blu-ray DVD.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2005.E2.80.932006:_Dissolution_of_the_Viacom_Entertainment_Group_and_Paramount">2005–2006: Dissolution of the Viacom Entertainment Group and Paramount</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2005–2006: Dissolution of the Viacom Entertainment Group and Paramount">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viacom" title="Viacom">Viacom</a></div>
<p>In 2005, Viacom announced the spinoff of CBS into a separate public entity. As part of this spinoff, the Entertainment Group that was led by Dolgen, Lansing and McGrath, was dissolved and Paramount broken up into its separate assets. Famous Music, part of the company since its founding by Jesse Lasky, was sold to Sony Music. The UPN network and its TV stations were transferred to CBS. Paramount itself was broken into two parts and the television production and assets were stripped and made part of CBS. The <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Parks" title="Paramount Parks">theme parks</a> group was sold to <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Fair" title="Cedar Fair">Cedar Fair</a> in 2006 and renamed the parks by taking out the "Paramount's" prefix. Simon and Schuster also became part of CBS. The company's three chains of movie theaters were divested – <a href="/wiki/Famous_Players" title="Famous Players">Famous Players</a> Theaters, the dominant theater circuit in Canada was sold to its competitor <a href="/wiki/Cineplex_Odeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Cineplex Odeon">Cineplex Odeon</a>. UCI which dominated the international theater markets consisting of 1,300+ screens in 11 countries was sold to buyout firm Terra Firma. Mann Theaters was slowly divested screen by screen with the world-famous "Graumann's Chinese Theater" being sold to a consortium led by Eli Samaha.</p>
<p>The resulting company, approximately 20% of its former size coalesced in 2006 under the leadership of its new CEO, Brad Grey who held the same title as Sherry Lansing despite the much smaller size of the business under his leadership.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2006.E2.80.93present:_Paramount_today">2006–present: Paramount today</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 2006–present: Paramount today">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="CBS_Corporation.2FViacom_split">CBS Corporation/Viacom split</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: CBS Corporation/Viacom split">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CBS_Corporation" title="CBS Corporation">CBS Corporation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg/220px-Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg/330px-Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg/440px-Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4440" data-file-height="3144" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramountpicturesmelrosegate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paramount Pictures' studio lot in Hollywood (Melrose Gate entrance)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reflecting in part the troubles of the broadcasting business, in 2006 Viacom wrote off over $18&#160;billion from its radio acquisitions and, early that year, announced that it would split itself in two. The split was completed in January 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the announcement of the split of Viacom, Dolgen and Lansing were replaced by former television executives Brad Grey and Gail Berman.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Viacom Inc. board split the company into CBS Corporation and a separate company under the Viacom name. The board scheduled the division for the first quarter of 2006. Under the plan, CBS Corp. would comprise CBS and UPN networks, Viacom Television Stations Group, Infinity Broadcasting, Viacom Outdoor, Paramount Television, KingWorld, Showtime, Simon and Schuster, Paramount Parks, and CBS News. The revamped Viacom would include "<a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>, <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon" title="Nickelodeon">Nickelodeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Entertainment_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Entertainment Television">BET</a> and several other cable networks as well as the Paramount movie studio".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Paramount's home entertainment unit continues to distribute the Paramount TV library through <a href="/wiki/CBS_Home_Entertainment" title="CBS Home Entertainment">CBS DVD</a>, as both Viacom and CBS Corporation are controlled by <a href="/wiki/Sumner_Redstone" title="Sumner Redstone">Sumner Redstone</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Amusements" title="National Amusements">National Amusements</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, CBS stopped using the Paramount name in its series and changed the name of the production arm to <a href="/wiki/CBS_Television_Studios" title="CBS Television Studios">CBS Television Studios</a>, eliminating the Paramount name from television, to distance itself from the latter.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DreamWorks_purchased">DreamWorks purchased</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: DreamWorks purchased">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On December 11, 2005, the Paramount Motion Pictures Group announced that it had purchased <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks" title="DreamWorks">DreamWorks</a> SKG (which was co-founded by former Paramount executive <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Katzenberg" title="Jeffrey Katzenberg">Jeffrey Katzenberg</a>) in a deal worth $1.6&#160;billion. The announcement was made by <a href="/wiki/Brad_Grey" title="Brad Grey">Brad Grey</a>, chairman and CEO of Paramount Pictures who noted that enhancing Paramount's pipeline of pictures is a "key strategic objective in restoring Paramount's stature as a leader in filmed entertainment."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The agreement does not include <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> SKG Inc., the most profitable part of the company that went public the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 6, 2008, DreamWorks executives announced that they were leaving Paramount and relaunching an independent DreamWorks. The DreamWorks trademarks remained with DreamWorks Animation when that company was spun off before the Paramount purchase, and DreamWorks Animation transferred the license to the name to the new company.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_since_2006">History since 2006</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History since 2006">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Grey also broke up the famous UIP international distribution company, the most successful international film distributor in history, after a 25-year partnership with Universal Studios and has started up a new international group. As a consequence Paramount fell from No.1 in the international markets to the lowest ranked major studio in 2006 but recovered in 2007.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>DreamWorks films, acquired by Paramount but still distributed internationally by Universal, are included in Paramount's market share. Grey also launched a Digital Entertainment division to take advantage of emerging digital distribution technologies. This led to Paramount becoming the second movie studio to sign a deal with <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a> to sell its films through the <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Also, in 2007, Paramount sold another one of its "heritage" units, <a href="/wiki/Famous_Music" title="Famous Music">Famous Music</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Sony/ATV_Music_Publishing" title="Sony/ATV Music Publishing">Sony/ATV Music Publishing</a> (best known for publishing many songs by <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, and for being co-owned by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>), ending a nearly-eight-decade run as a division of Paramount, being the studio's music publishing arm since the period when the entire company went by the name "Famous Players."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 2008, Paramount partnered with Los Angeles-based developer <a href="/wiki/FanRocket" title="FanRocket">FanRocket</a> to make short scenes taken from its film library available to users on Facebook. The application, called VooZoo, allows users to send movie clips to other Facebook users and to post clips on their profile pages.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Paramount engineered a similar deal with <a href="/wiki/Makena_Technologies" class="mw-redirect" title="Makena Technologies">Makena Technologies</a> to allow users of <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">vMTV</a> and <a href="/wiki/There.com" class="mw-redirect" title="There.com">There.com</a> to view and send movie clips.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2010, Paramount founded <a href="/wiki/Insurge_Pictures" title="Insurge Pictures">Insurge Pictures</a>, an independent distributor of "micro budget" films. The distributor planned ten movies with budgets of $100,000 each.<sup id="cite_ref-ip_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ip-59">[59]</a></sup> The first release was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Devil_Inside_(film)" title="The Devil Inside (film)">The Devil Inside</a></i>, a movie with a budget of about US$1 million.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In March 2015, following waning box office returns, Paramount shuttered Insurge Pictures and moved its operations to the main studio.</p>
<p>In July 2011, in the wake of critical and box office success of the animated feature, <i><a href="/wiki/Rango_(2011_film)" title="Rango (2011 film)">Rango</a></i>, and the departure of DreamWorks Animation upon completion of their distribution contract in 2012, Paramount announced the formation of a new division, devoted to the creation of animated productions.<sup id="cite_ref-pa_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pa-61">[61]</a></sup> It marks Paramount's return to having its own animated division for the first time since 1967, when Paramount Cartoon Studios shut down (it was formerly <a href="/wiki/Famous_Studios" title="Famous Studios">Famous Studios</a> until 1956).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2013, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(division)" title="Walt Disney Studios (division)">Walt Disney Studios</a> (via its <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">parent company</a>'s purchase of <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm" title="Lucasfilm">Lucasfilm, Ltd.</a> a year earlier<sup id="cite_ref-Schou_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schou-63">[63]</a></sup>) purchased Paramount's remaining distribution and marketing rights to future <i><a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones_(franchise)" title="Indiana Jones (franchise)">Indiana Jones</a></i> films, while Paramount will permanently retain the distribution rights to the first four films, and will receive "financial participation" from any additional films.<sup id="cite_ref-VarDisney_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VarDisney-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LATDisney_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATDisney-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-THRDisney_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THRDisney-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2016, Viacom CEO and newly appointed chairman <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Dauman" title="Philippe Dauman">Philippe Dauman</a> announced that the conglomerate is in talks to find an investor to purchase a minority stake in Paramount.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Sumner Redstone and his daughter Shari are reportedly opposed with the deal.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> On July 13, 2016, <a href="/wiki/Wanda_Group" title="Wanda Group">Wanda Group</a> was in talks to acquire a 49% stake of Paramount.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The talks with Wanda were dropped. On January 19, 2017, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Film_Group_Corp." class="mw-redirect" title="Shanghai Film Group Corp.">Shanghai Film Group Corp.</a> and Huahua Media said they would finance at least 25% of all Paramount Pictures movies over a three-year period. Shanghai Film Group and Huahua Media, in the deal, would help distribute and market Paramount's features in China. At the time, the <i>Wall Street Journal</i> wrote that "nearly every major Hollywood studio has a co-financing deal with a Chinese company."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 27, 2017, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Gianopulos" title="Jim Gianopulos">Jim Gianopulos</a> was named as a chairman and CEO of Paramount Pictures, replacing <a href="/wiki/Brad_Grey" title="Brad Grey">Brad Grey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Investments">Investments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Investments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DreamWorks_Pictures">DreamWorks Pictures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: DreamWorks Pictures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2006, Paramount became the parent of DreamWorks Pictures. Soros Strategic Partners and Dune Entertainment II soon afterwards acquired controlling interest in live-action films released through DreamWorks, with the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Just_Like_Heaven_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Just Like Heaven (film)">Just Like Heaven</a></i> on September 16, 2005. The remaining live-action films released until March 2006 remained under direct Paramount control. However, Paramount still owns distribution and other ancillary rights to Soros and Dune films.</p>
<p>On February 8, 2010, Viacom repurchased Soros' controlling stake in DreamWorks' library of films released before 2005 for around $400 million.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Even as DreamWorks switched distribution of live-action films not part of existing franchises to <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_Motion_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures">Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a>, Paramount continues to own the films released before the merger, and the films that Paramount themselves distributed, including <a href="/wiki/Sequel" title="Sequel">sequel</a> rights such as that of <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Fockers" title="Little Fockers">Little Fockers</a></i> (2011), distributed by Paramount and DreamWorks. It was a <a href="/wiki/Sequel" title="Sequel">sequel</a> to two existing <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks" title="DreamWorks">DreamWorks</a> films, <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Parents" title="Meet the Parents">Meet the Parents</a></i> (2000) and <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Fockers" title="Meet the Fockers">Meet the Fockers</a></i> (2004). Paramount only owned the international distribution rights to <i>Little Fockers</i>, whereas <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a> handled domestic distribution<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>Paramount owned distribution rights to the <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> library of films made before 2013, and their previous distribution deal with future DWA titles expired at the end of 2012, with <i><a href="/wiki/Rise_of_the_Guardians" title="Rise of the Guardians">Rise of the Guardians</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> took over distribution on post-2012 titles beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Croods" title="The Croods">The Croods</a></i> (2013)<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and will end with <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_Underpants:_The_First_Epic_Movie" title="Captain Underpants: The First Epic Movie">Captain Underpants: The First Epic Movie</a></i> (2017) with <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Pictures</a> taking over the distribution deal with DreamWorks Animation due to <a href="/wiki/NBCUniversal" title="NBCUniversal">NBCUniversal</a>'s acquisition of DreamWorks Animation in 2016, starting in 2019 with the release of <i><a href="/wiki/How_To_Train_Your_Dragon" class="mw-redirect" title="How To Train Your Dragon">How To Train Your Dragon</a> 3</i>, though Paramount's rights to pre-2013 DreamWorks Animation films would've expired 16 years after each film's initial theatrical release date.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> However, in July 2014, <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> purchased Paramount's distribution rights to the pre-2013 library, with DreamWorks Animation's current distributor <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> starting to distribute the library.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another asset of the former DreamWorks owned by Paramount, is the pre-2008 <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Television" title="DreamWorks Television">DreamWorks Television</a> library, distributed through Paramount Worldwide Television Licensing &amp; Distribution, the library includes <a href="/wiki/Spin_City" title="Spin City">Spin City</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_Incident" title="High Incident">High Incident</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freaks_and_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Freaks and Greeks">Freaks and Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Undeclared" title="Undeclared">Undeclared</a> and <a href="/wiki/On_the_Lot" title="On the Lot">On the Lot</a>, the DreamWorks Television library was distributed by the old <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television" title="Paramount Television">Paramount Television</a> years before.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CBS_library">CBS library</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: CBS library">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Independent company Hollywood Classics now represents Paramount with the theatrical distribution of all the films produced by the various motion picture divisions of <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> over the years, as a result of the Viacom/CBS merger.</p>
<p>Paramount (via <a href="/wiki/CBS_Home_Entertainment" title="CBS Home Entertainment">CBS Home Entertainment</a>) has outright video distribution to the aforementioned CBS library with few exceptions-for example, the original <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twilight_Zone" title="The Twilight Zone">Twilight Zone</a></i> DVDs are handled by <a href="/wiki/Image_Entertainment" title="Image Entertainment">Image Entertainment</a>. Until 2009, the video rights to <i><a href="/wiki/My_Fair_Lady_(film)" title="My Fair Lady (film)">My Fair Lady</a></i> were with original theatrical distributor <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a>, under license from CBS (the video license to that film has now reverted to CBS Home Entertainment under Paramount).</p>
<p>The CBS-produced/owned films, unlike other films in Paramount's library, are still distributed by CBS Television Distribution on TV, and not by Trifecta Entertainment &amp; Media, because CBS (or a subdivision) is the copyright holder for these films.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units">Units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
