<p><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_interests">Other interests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Other interests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2012, Paramount licensed their name and logo to a luxury hotel investment group which subsequently named the company Paramount Hotels and Resorts. The investors plan to build 50 hotels throughout the world based on the themes of Hollywood and the California lifestyle. Among the features are private screening rooms and the Paramount library available in the hotel rooms. On April 2013, Paramount Hotels and Dubai-based <a href="/wiki/DAMAC_Properties" title="DAMAC Properties">DAMAC Properties</a> announced the building of the first resort: "DAMAC Towers by Paramount."<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Logo">Logo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Logo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg/250px-DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg" width="250" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg/375px-DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg/500px-DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2799" data-file-height="1749" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DarioCampanile.Paramount.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist Dario Campanile poses with a picture Paramount commissioned him to paint for its 75th anniversary in 1987. The company later used the painting as a basis for its new logo. That logo was introduced as a prototype in the 1986 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Child" title="The Golden Child">The Golden Child</a></i>; the 1987 film <i><a href="/wiki/Critical_Condition_(film)" title="Critical Condition (film)">Critical Condition</a></i> was the first to feature the finalized version of the logo. 1999's <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park:_Bigger,_Longer_%26_Uncut" title="South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut">South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut</a></i> was the first to use an enhanced version of the logo, which was last used on 2002's <i><a href="/wiki/Crossroads_(2002_film)" title="Crossroads (2002 film)">Crossroads</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_Pictures_logo_(2002).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Paramount_Pictures_logo_%282002%29.jpg/250px-Paramount_Pictures_logo_%282002%29.jpg" width="250" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Paramount_Pictures_logo_%282002%29.jpg/375px-Paramount_Pictures_logo_%282002%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/94/Paramount_Pictures_logo_%282002%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="490" data-file-height="204" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paramount_Pictures_logo_(2002).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
For its 90th anniversary, Paramount adopted the logo shown here. In 2012, it was used in tandem with the current one. This picture shows the 2010 modification of the logo, which includes <a href="/wiki/Viacom" title="Viacom">Viacom</a>'s new byline introduced in 2006. The first movie to use the new Viacom byline was <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_2" title="Iron Man 2">Iron Man 2</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The distinctively pyramidal Paramount mountain has been the company's logo since its inception and is the oldest surviving Hollywood film logo. In the sound era, the logo was accompanied by a fanfare called <i><a href="/wiki/Paramount_on_Parade" title="Paramount on Parade">Paramount on Parade</a></i> after the film of the same name, released in 1930. The words to the fanfare, originally sung in the 1930 film, were "Proud of the crowd that will never be loud, it's Paramount on Parade."</p>
<p>Legend has it that the mountain is based on a doodle made by <a href="/wiki/W._W._Hodkinson" class="mw-redirect" title="W. W. Hodkinson">W. W. Hodkinson</a> during a meeting with <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Zukor" title="Adolph Zukor">Adolph Zukor</a>. It is said to be based on the memories of his childhood in <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>. Some claim that Utah's <a href="/wiki/Ben_Lomond_Mountain_(Utah)" title="Ben Lomond Mountain (Utah)">Ben Lomond</a> is the mountain Hodkinson doodled, and that Peru's <a href="/wiki/Artesonraju" title="Artesonraju">Artesonraju</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> is the mountain in the live-action logo, while others claim that the Italian side of <a href="/wiki/Monte_Viso" title="Monte Viso">Monviso</a> inspired the logo. Some editions of the logo bear a striking resemblance to the <a href="/wiki/Pfeifferhorn" title="Pfeifferhorn">Pfeifferhorn</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> another <a href="/wiki/Wasatch_Range" title="Wasatch Range">Wasatch Range</a> peak.</p>
<p>The motion picture logo has gone through many changes over the years:</p>
<ul>
<li>The logo began as a somewhat indistinct charcoal rendering of the mountain ringed with superimposed stars. The logo originally had twenty-four stars, as a tribute to the then current system of contracts for actors, since Paramount had twenty-four stars signed at the time.</li>
<li>In 1951, the logo was redesigned as a <a href="/wiki/Matte_painting" title="Matte painting">matte painting</a> created by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Domela" title="Jan Domela">Jan Domela</a>.</li>
<li>A newer, more realistic-looking logo debuted in 1953 for Paramount films made in 3D. It was reworked in early-to-mid 1954 for Paramount films made in <a href="/wiki/Widescreen" title="Widescreen">widescreen</a> process <a href="/wiki/VistaVision" title="VistaVision">VistaVision</a>. The text <i>VistaVision – Motion Picture High Fidelity</i> was often imposed over the Paramount logo briefly before dissolving into the <a href="/wiki/Title_sequence" title="Title sequence">title sequence</a>. In early 1968, the text "A Paramount Picture/Release" was shortened to "Paramount", and the byline <i>A Gulf+Western Company</i> appeared on the bottom. The logo was given yet another modification in 1974, with the number of stars being reduced to 22, and the Paramount text and Gulf+Western byline appearing in different fonts.</li>
<li>In September 1975, the logo was simplified in a shade of blue, adopting the modified design of the 1968 print logo, which was in use for many decades afterward.</li>
<li>The studio launched an entirely new logo in December 1986 with <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated imagery</a> of a lake and stars. This version of the Paramount logo was designed by Dario Campanile and animated by Apogee, Inc; for this logo, the stars would move across the screen into the arc shape instead of it being superimposed over the mountain as it was before. An redone version of this logo debuted with <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park:_Bigger,_Longer_%26_Uncut" title="South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut">South Park: Bigger, Longer &amp; Uncut</a></i> (released on June 30, 1999).</li>
<li>In March 2002, an updated logo was introduced in which shooting stars would fall from a night sky to form the arc while the Paramount logo would fly into place between them. An enhanced version of this logo debuted with <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_2" title="Iron Man 2">Iron Man 2</a></i>, released on May 7, 2010. The south col area of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> became the primary basis. The music is accompanied by <i>Paramount on Parade</i>. This logo is still featured on DVD and Blu-ray releases with <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(original)" title="Viacom (original)">Old Viacom</a> Byline.</li>
<li>On December 16, 2011, an updated logo<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> was introduced with animation done by Devastudios, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The new logo includes a surrounding mountain range and the sun shining in the background. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Giacchino" title="Michael Giacchino">Michael Giacchino</a> composed the logo's new fanfare.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Studio_tours">Studio tours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Studio tours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Those wishing to visit Paramount can take studio tours, which are offered seven days a week. Reservations are required, and can be made by visiting the tour website.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Each tour day is different because the tour has to work around active movie sets. Pictures are allowed in some areas. Your tour guide will let you know if you can not take pictures. Most of the time this is because an area or studio is "set", meaning the area or set is being used for a current production and can't be photographed due to copyright laws. The regular tour offers a behind-the-scenes look at the current operations of the studio. Most of the buildings on the tour are named for historical Paramount executives or the artists that worked at Paramount over the years. Many of the stars' dressing rooms have been converted into working offices. The stages where <i><a href="/wiki/Samson_and_Delilah_(1949_film)" title="Samson and Delilah (1949 film)">Samson and Delilah</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sunset_Boulevard_(film)" title="Sunset Boulevard (film)">Sunset Blvd.</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/White_Christmas_(film)" title="White Christmas (film)">White Christmas</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rear_Window" title="Rear Window">Rear Window</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sabrina_(1954_film)" title="Sabrina (1954 film)">Sabrina</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Breakfast_at_Tiffany%27s_(film)" title="Breakfast at Tiffany's (film)">Breakfast at Tiffany's</a></i>, and many other classic films were shot are still in use today. The studio's backlot set, "New York Street", features numerous blocks of façades that depict a number of New York locales: "Washington Square" (where some scenes in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Heiress" title="The Heiress">The Heiress</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Olivia_de_Havilland" title="Olivia de Havilland">Olivia de Havilland</a>, were shot), "Brooklyn", "Financial District", and others. Led by a guide on a golf cart, the tour takes approximately two hours. The best time to take the regular tour is on the weekends because most of the filming is shut down and you can get into more areas. The VIP tour takes you to additional areas not covered by the standard tour, plus you have lunch in the studio restaurant. This tour takes 5 to 6 hours and is usually only offered on weekdays.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_library">Film library</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paramount_Pictures&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Film library">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Paramount_Pictures_films" title="List of Paramount Pictures films">List of Paramount Pictures films</a></div>
<p>A few years after the ruling of the <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Paramount_Pictures,_Inc." title="United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.">United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.</a></i> case in 1948, Music Corporation of America (<a href="/wiki/MCA_Inc." title="MCA Inc.">MCA</a>) approached Paramount offering $50 million for 750 sound feature films released prior to December 1, 1949 with payment to be spread over a period of several years. Paramount saw this as a bargain since the fleeting movie studio saw very little value in its library of old films at the time. To address any anti-trust concerns, MCA set up EMKA, Ltd. as a dummy corporation to sell these films to television. EMKA's/Universal Pictures library includes the five Paramount <a href="/wiki/Marx_Brothers" title="Marx Brothers">Marx Brothers</a> films, most of the Bob Hope–Bing Crosby <i><a href="/wiki/Road_to..." class="mw-redirect" title="Road to...">Road to...</a></i> pictures, and other classics such as <i><a href="/wiki/Trouble_in_Paradise_(film)" title="Trouble in Paradise (film)">Trouble in Paradise</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Express_(film)" title="Shanghai Express (film)">Shanghai Express</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/She_Done_Him_Wrong" title="She Done Him Wrong">She Done Him Wrong</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sullivan%27s_Travels" title="Sullivan's Travels">Sullivan's Travels</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Palm_Beach_Story" title="The Palm Beach Story">The Palm Beach Story</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/For_Whom_the_Bell_Tolls_(film)" title="For Whom the Bell Tolls (film)">For Whom the Bell Tolls</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Double_Indemnity_(film)" title="Double Indemnity (film)">Double Imdemnity</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Weekend_(film)" title="The Lost Weekend (film)">The Lost Weekend</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Heiress" title="The Heiress">The Heiress</a></i>.</p>

<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Note_1"><b><a href="#ref_Note_1">^</a></b></span> North America distribution only. Released by <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> internationally.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Note_2"><b><a href="#ref_Note_2">^</a></b></span> In July 2014, the film's distribution rights were purchased by <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> from Paramount and transferred to <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Note_3"><b><a href="#ref_Note_3">^</a></b></span> In July 2013, the film's distribution rights were transferred from Paramount to the <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Studios_(division)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Walt Disney Studios (division)">Walt Disney Studios</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
</div>

<ul>

<li>Dick, Bernard F. <i>Engulfed: the death of Paramount Pictures and the birth of corporate Hollywood</i>. Lexington, Kentucky: University of Press Kentucky Scholarly, 2001.</li>
<li>Eames, John Douglas, with additional text by Robert Abele. <i>The Paramount Story: The Complete History of the Studio and Its Films</i>. New York: Simon &amp; Schuster, 2002.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Evans_(producer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Evans (producer)">Evans, Robert</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kid_Stays_in_the_Picture" title="The Kid Stays in the Picture">The Kid Stays in the Picture</a></i>. New York: Hyperion Press, 1994.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neal_Gabler" title="Neal Gabler">Gabler, Neal</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/An_Empire_of_Their_Own:_How_the_Jews_Invented_Hollywood" class="mw-redirect" title="An Empire of Their Own: How the Jews Invented Hollywood">An Empire of Their Own: How the Jews Invented Hollywood</a></i>. New York: Crown Publishers, 1988.</li>
<li>Lasky, Jesse L. with Don Weldon, <i>I Blow My Own Horn</i>. Garden City NY: Doubleday, 1957.</li>
<li>Mordden, Ethan. <i>The Hollywood Studios</i>. New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 1988.</li>
<li>Schatz, Thomas. <i>The Genius of the System.</i> New York: Pantheon, 1988.</li>
<li>Sklar, Robert. <i>Movie-Made America</i>. New York: Vintage, 1989.</li>
<li>Zukor, Adolph, with Dale Kramer. <i>The Public Is Never Wrong: The Autobiography of Adolph Zukor.</i> New York: G.P. Putnam's Sons, 1953.</li>
</ul>
